As\nb\na result, the wind field cannotbedetermined\nfromthe pressureor height. Moreover,\na reliableestimateofthe divergentcomponent f the wind is necessary depictthe\no\nto\nconvectiveareasin the Tropics that provide a sourceof energyfor the equatorial\nregionsand at times the mid-latitudes. In\ncumulonimbus\nclouds,thewatercontentscanexceed gm-3.\n5\nIn addition to water droplets,clouds often contain ice particles when the\ntemperature is low. However, observations show that the 0\xc2\xb0C level in the\natmospheredoesnot induce a sharp discontinuityin the microstructureof clouds\nand precipitation. Clouds have a high probability of containing no ice if the\ntemperature of the cloud is warmer than -10\xc2\xb0C. However, or measurementof Doppler\nf\nshift, the lidar onlyneedsonepulsewhile the radarsystemneeds64 or morepulses.\nThus the radar systemonlyhas a slight advantage prf overthe lidar systemwith\non\nthe current technology.\n\n-72-\n\nChapter\n\n4\n\nEstimation\nSpectrum\n\n4.1\n\nMOMENTS\n\nIn Doppler\n\nradar\n\nof the power\nechoed\n\nof\n\nof\n\nPower\n\nOF SPECTRUM\nsignal\n\nspectra\n\nsignal,\n\nMoments\n\nprocessing,\n\nof radar\n\niare often\n\nechoes.\n\nm i, is defined\n\nrequired\n\nAn ith moment\n\nto estimates\n\nof the moments\n\nof the power\n\nspectral\n\ndensity\n\nof\n\nas:\n\n+OO\n\nffi S(f) df\no00\n\n(4.1)\n\nm.\n1\n\n+oo\n\nJ\'S(f) df\n.00\n\nwhere\nradars,\n\nS(f) is the power\n\nspectral\n\nonly the first three\n(1) mean\n\npower\n\nsignal\n\nenergy\n\nreturned\n\nzeroth\n\nmoment\n\nreflect\n\nthe attenuation\n\nuseful\n\nfor deriving\n\ndensity\n\nmoments\n\nof the\nfrom\n\nis proportional\ncaused\n\nor zeroth\n\ntarget.\nto the\n\necho\n\nmoment\n\nwhich\n\nFor most\n\nindicates\n\nweather\n\nfrom\n\nHowever,\n\ntotal\n\nclouds,\n\nthe\n\nand\n\nhydrometeors,\n\nthe\n\nfrom\n\nmeasurements\n\nor precipitation.\n\nrate.\n\nsignal.\n\n[44]:\n\nIn wind\n\nby clouds\n\nthe precipitation\n\nof the\n\nare important\n\nsignal\nthe\n\nfunction\n\nit may\n\nalso\n\nThis information\n\nit does not directly\n\nmay be\n\nrelate\n\nto the\n\nwinds.\n\n(2) the\nspectral\n\ndensity\n\nan essential\naverage\n\nmean\n\nradial\n\nDoppler\n\nis a measure\n\nparameter\n\nin the\n\nvelocity\n\nof wind\n\nvelocity\n\nor the\n\nof the radial\nestimation\n\nfirst moment\n\ncomponent\nof wind\n\nfields.\n\n-73-\n\nof the normalized\n\nof the target\nvectors,\n\nand\n\nvelocity.\n\nreflects\n\npower\nThis\n\na weighted\n\nis\n\n(3) The secondmomentis useful for derivingthe root-mean-square(RMS)\nwidth of the power spectrum (or spectralwidth). It is a measureof the velocity\ndispersion,i.e., the shearor turbulencewithin a resolutionvolume.\nIn the study of airborne radar wind sensors, the first moment is the\nfundamental variable for deriving the Doppler shift caused by the wind-driven\ndropletsin clouds. The spectralwidth derivedfromthe first and the secondmoment\nmay be useful for determiningthe error in estimatesof the first moment. In this\nchapter, we discuss the algorithms for estimating the first moment of the power\nspectral density. Monte Carlo simulations were applied to evaluatethe secondorder statistics of the algorithms discussedin this chapter. Since all of the\nestimatorsare functionsof the SNRand the spectralwidth, results of the computer\nsimulations are presentedwith the RMSerrors as functions of SNRsand spectral\nwidths. The discussionand the computersimulations are basedon normalized\nfrequencies\nwhich rangefrom -1 to 1. However, he results can be easilyconverted\nt\nto a specific application like RAWS,by multiplying the results with the Nyquist\nfrequency.\n\n4.2\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\n4.2.1\n\nRANDOM\nBefore\n\nfrequency\nprocesses\n\nand their\n\nthe\n\nlet the\n\nreceiver,\n\noverlaid\n\nechoes\n\nrange\n\nambiguities.\n\nRONSARD\n\nradar\n\n[84] using\n\nin the\n\ntransmitter.\n\nof the mean\n\nphases\n\nfor removing\n\nusing\n\nThe\n\nfrequency\nthem\n\n- 116-\n\ntrips\n\ndue\n\nfrom the\nwere\n\ncoding\n\nto overlying\n\nto compensate\n\nonly the echoes\nfrom other\n\nphase\n\nfor the\nfirst\n\nincoherent\n\nwas\n\nthe\nused\n\nechoes\n\nby\n\nphases\n\nof\n\ntrip were\nand\n\nA\n\nmade\n\nappeared\n\nas increasedwhite noise. Therefore,the bias in the estimate of mean frequency\ncausedby interference\ncouldbe reduced.\nLaird suggested phase-coding\na\nschemein which the phaseof the transmitted\npulse is coded with binary random number 0 or rc[85l. By employinga coherent\nreference,this schemecould re-cohereradar echoesfrom any particular trip and\nmake the echoesfrom other trips appear like white noise. However,becausethe\nphase coding method only spreads the energy of interference echoes in the\nfrequencydomain, and doesnot reducethe total energyof the interferenceechoes,\nonly one trip can provide a sufficiently large SNRfor reliable measurement. As a\nresult, the random phasecodingmethodhas limited use.\nTherefore,the random phase-codingschemecannot effectivelyrecoverthe\nechoes\nfrom a trip ff the energyof interfering echoes\nfrom other trips is strong. For\nthis reason. Siggia developedan adaptive filter for processingthe random phase\ncoded radar signal [86]. The adaptive filter can reduce the effect of interfering\nechoesand increasethe effectiveSNR. However,a significant improvementcan be\nachievedonlywhen the spectrumof the radar echohas a narrowwidth [87],and this\nmethod is computationally intense.\nComputer simulations of ambiguity functions for three different phasecodedpulse trains werepresentedin Figures5.2, 5.3 and 5.4. Figure5.2 showsthe\nambiguity function of a uniformly spacedpulse train with no phase coding. As\nexpected, the uncoded pulse train generates periodic range and frequency\nambiguities. Figure 5.3 showsthe ambiguity function of a uniformly spacedpulse\ntrain with random phase coding as used in [85]. We can see that the range\nambiguitiesare reduced,the energyof interferencesis distributed acrossthe whole\nNyqulst interval and acts like white noise. However,the frequencyambiguitiesare\nunchanged. A similar statement can be applied to Figure 5.4, which showsthe\nambiguity function of a uniformly spacedpulse train modulatedwith a 13-bit long\nBarker-code.\n\n- symmetric\n\nfrom\n\nnon-symmetric\n\nderived\nand\n\nhas\n\nthe transmitter,\n\nor\n\nspectrum\n\nor\n\nThe performance of the FFT estimator and the AR-based estimators are\naffected by the noise and alias of the frequency of the radar echoes. A noisesuppressionmethodwas discussedin this chapter. It can effectivelyremove bias\nthe\ncausedby white noise. Wealsodiscusseda methodfor removingthe bias causedby\nfrequencyalias; this methodcan alsoreducethe biascausedby noise.\nThe FFT, the covarianceestimator,and the AR estimatorare comparablein\nterms of estimateerrors. Although,the RMSerrors in the estimatesdependon both\nthe spectral widths and SNRsof the input signals. The RMS error is mainly a\nfunction of the spectral width of the signal when the SNR is above 5 dB. As\ndiscussed,the spectralwidths of the signalsin RAWSare about half of the Nyquist\ninterval. In such case,the RMS errors producedby these estimatorsdiscussedin\nChapter4 wereaslargeas 10%of the Nyquistfrequency.That is, for a 3500-HzPRF,\nthe RMSerrors are around 175Hz. This error is higher than the error limit (23 Hz)\nfor the mean frequencyestimatesneededto produce the lm s"1 accuracyin wind\nestimates. About 64 independentmeasurements\nmay needto be averaged achieve\nto\nthe requiredaccuracy.\n(6) In Chapter 5, we reviewedseveral algorithms for reducing the radar\nambiguities,such as a phase-coding\nmethodand staggered methods. However,\nprf\nthesemethodsdo not performwell when the spectralwidths of the radar echoesare\nlarge ( > 50% of the Nyquist interval]. Therefore,a new method based on using\ndifferent waveformmodulations on successive\ntransmitted pulses was developed\nto\nreducethe radar ambiguities. Monte-Carlosimulations were used to comparethe\nperformanceof this method with two staggered-prfmethods.The results showed\nthat when the spectral width of the signal increases, the RMS errors increase\nrapidly with the staggered-prfmethods. However,the RMS error for the waveform\nmodulation method does not changevery much as the the spectral width of the\nradar signal increases.\nIn the RAWS\nsystem,the spectralwidths of the radar echoes abouthalf of\nare\nthe Nyquist interval. In such a case,the staggeredPRF-Amethod has very large\nprobability of falsemeasurements; about 20%at 10 dB SNRand 30% at 5 dB SNR.\n- 161-\n\nThe RMSerrors producedby the waveformmodulation are about 7% of the Nyquist\ninterval at 10 dB SNR.and about 12% at 5 dB SN\'I_ The staggeredPRF-Bmethod\ngenerates very large RMS errors, over 50% of the Nyquist interval, when the\nspectral width is equal to or greaterthan half of the Nyquist interval. In all these\ncomputersimulations,the extendedmaximumunambiguousfrequencyis 7 times as\nlarge as the Nyquist interval, For a 3500-Hz prf. the m_irnum unambiguous\nfrequency is 12250Hz. In summary, the waveformmodulation method is the most\npromising algorithm amongthe algorithms discussedin this chapter to be used in\nthe RAWSfor estimatingthe Dopplermeanfrequency.\n\n-162-\n\nREFERENCES\n[1] G.
